I live in argentina, and here "Blue raspberry" it's not really a thing. Not even our sour patch kids ripoff   uses it. It uses blueberry, Bubblegum, and lemon (it only comes in three colors) In fact, raspberry as a whole is not as popular as a flavor here. I've only ever seen it being used *twice* in candy. Only in Jello and Push-pops. But it's often asociated with pink. Strawberry is often red, Grape is purple, Watermelon is always green *and* pink.  "But then, which one is blue?" You might be wondering. For some reason that I'll never understand, *our* version of blue raspberry is pineapple. For some reason, argentine pineapple candy is blue. So for you, Blue tastes like raspberry, to me it tastes like Pineapple.

The reason grape doesn't taste like grape is probably because grape juice ferments really, really easily. It's part of the reason why wine was one of the first alcoholic beverages to be discovered. We didn't have the power to serve straight-up grape juice as a beverage until the combination of increased chemistry knowledge and the growing temperance movement propelled it into being in the Victorian era. So it's no wonder that when making grape candies, people would not want to work with something that very easily turns into wine.

Blueberries and many other fruits (grapes, cherries, raspberriesâ€¦) contain anthocyanin, a molecule that is a reddish colour in its naturally acidic environment: fruit. But, when it comes into contact with alkaline ingredients (the baking soda in a cake batter, for ex.), the anthocyanin turns blue.
Actually the reason the raspberry flavor is blue is because raspberries turn blue whenccooked with baking soda, just learn that this is a similar thing that happens to sunflower seeds.

blue
Blue raspberry is a manufactured flavoring and food coloring for candy, snack foods, syrups, and soft drinks.[1][2] The flavor and color do not derive from any species of raspberry.[1][2] According to Jerry Bowman, executive director of the Flavor & Extract Manufacturers Association of the United States as of 2016, the flavor profile of raspberry was developed using "mostly esters of the banana, cherry, and pineapple variety."[2] Sugar is commonly used to create taste appeal for the blue raspberry flavoring.[2]

This is so widely different from where I live in the EU. I don't know if it's the same in other EU countries, but here grape and cherry are really rare flavours, and I've never seen blue raspberry before at all! Strawberry is usually red, raspberries get a dark pink colour, orange is for oranges, yellow is usually for lemon (but may be for banana), green is kind of rare but is usually apple (or sometimes lime), blueberries get a dark blue, and a dark purple is for blackberries. If there is a cherry flavour, it will get red instead of strawberry, and strawberry will get a bright pink instead, otherwise a bright pink can be bubblegum. Sweets that only come in raspberry flavour (an example would be a popular valentines day treat which consists of raspberry flavoured gelatin hearts) are usually red, although not quite as bright as strawberry/cherry. I've never tried grape flavouring before, but I have seen it in stores a few times, where it's always a bright purple.

The classic grape flavor is mimicking Concord grapes a much more flavorful kind of grape that actually tastes similar to artificial grape candy and what the candy is mimicking.
I never heard of blue raspberry being referred to blue stuff here in Brazil, even for Gatorade, they always market as either tutti frutti flavor (which yes, can just as often come in pink) or as cotton candy flavor (and the idea of that flavor is just ingrained now in public conscious). The third option to describe blue flavors here usually is "blue sky, but it's usually closer to the cotton candy flavor. But thrn again the reason we don't associtate blue artificial flavors to a blue raspberry here in Brazil is because raspberry itself is not a common fruit flavor here at all, usually present only in imported products, you're far more likely to find some local fruit like "aÃ§aÃ­" as a flavor than both red or blue raspberry. All that to say that when you asked me what blue flavor was I thought of a """natural""" fruit mix like tutti frutti or poli frutti instead of a single thing like a banana, cherry, grape or a strawberry.

Actually, there are blue raspberries, and not Rubus Leucudermis but Rubus caesius. They are actually blue and grown in northern Europe but are exceedingly rare except on the island of Gotland (who calls them SalmbÃ¤r or Salmberry, not to be confused with salmonberry which is a very different thing). The local specialty is saffron pancakes with blue raspberry jam, it is darn tasty.  It however taste nothing like the so called "blue raspberry" in candy and soft drinks but they are real, I have eaten them. They are close to blueberries in color, sometimes a bit darker. There are of course some garden variety blueberries that are blue too, but they don't taste the same.

It is normally you eat with your eyes first, then your sense of smell, then depending on the type of food sound, then mouth feel and then the last is taste. What you perceive for taste is mostly by what you see and smell. When you take once sense away from a food that food will taste different. Sound does make a difference in flavor and texture. For example, take a pretzel for instance and find lets say 3 sounds, first eat the pretzel without sound to get a base taste and texture, then play that sound of choice (I had chosen falling tree sound, falling rocks, and gravel.) and notice how each and different sound changes the flavor and texture of that pretzel. You are eating the same substance but your brain thinks different. I find it fascinating how your senses can change what you perceive when you take one sense out.

Otter Pops Lore/Adventures:  Alexander the Grape: Alexander is the leader of the Otter Pop crew. He is a smooth talker and loves to be the center of attention. He is always dressed in a purple suit and is known for his grape-flavored Otter Pop.  Little Orphan Orange: Orphan Orange is a cute and spunky character who loves to have fun. She is always dressed in orange and has a sweet, tangy flavor.  Sir Isaac Lime: Sir Isaac is a sophisticated and intelligent character who is always dressed in green. He has a tart, lime flavor and is known for his sharp wit.  Poncho Punch: Poncho is a laid-back character who loves to have a good time. He is always dressed in yellow and has a tropical fruit flavor.  Louie-Bloo Raspberry: Louie is a mischievous character who loves to play pranks on his friends. He is always dressed in blue and has a sweet, raspberry flavor.  Together, these characters make up the Otter Pop crew, and they are often featured in advertising and promotional materials for the brand. While there is no official cinematic universe or overarching storyline associated with Otter Pops, each character has its own unique personality and backstory that fans can appreciate.  The Otter Pop crew, Alexander the Grape, Little Orphan Orange, Sir Isaac Lime, Poncho Punch, and Louie-Bloo Raspberry have gone on many adventures together over the years. Here are a few examples of their imaginative escapades:  The Otter Pop Olympics: The Otter Pop crew decides to host their own Olympics, with each character competing in their own event. Alexander the Grape shows off his speed in the Grape Dash, Little Orphan Orange showcases her gymnastic skills in the Orange Beam, Sir Isaac Lime wows the judges with his synchronized swimming routine, Poncho Punch takes on the surfing competition, and Louie-Bloo Raspberry uses his mischievousness to win the Blue Ribbon for practical jokes.  The Great Otter Pop Rescue: The Otter Pop crew hears about a group of stranded animals on a nearby island and sets off on a rescue mission. They work together to build a boat, navigate treacherous waters, and use their unique talents to save the stranded creatures. In the end, they make it back home safely, and the animals are reunited with their families.  Otter Pop Detective Agency: The Otter Pop crew transforms into detectives, working together to solve a mystery in their neighborhood. Alexander the Grape takes on the role of the mastermind, Little Orphan Orange is the undercover agent, Sir Isaac Lime uses his sharp mind to analyze clues, Poncho Punch is the lookout, and Louie-Bloo Raspberry is the sneaky one who gathers evidence. Together, they crack the case and bring the culprit to justice.  Otter Pop Space Adventure: The Otter Pop crew blasts off into space, exploring new galaxies and encountering all sorts of strange creatures. Alexander the Grape takes on the role of captain, Little Orphan Orange becomes the ship's engineer, Sir Isaac Lime is the navigator, Poncho Punch cooks up delicious space meals, and Louie-Bloo Raspberry is the one who makes first contact with the alien life forms they encounter. They discover new worlds and have many adventures before returning home to Earth.

So bizarre to think Matpat didn't pick blackberries for a blue raspberry in real life. Are they widespread in the USA?   In Russia, we grow them in our summer gardens, but they are harder to grow than raspberries, and there are always fewer of them, so they are treasured by children as one of the coolest berries in the garden. So any blackberry-flavored candy is usually highly appreciated.  Thinking of that... in the USSR almost every family was given a nice piece of land for free not too far from their towns. People were usually building cheap summer houses there and used them as a place to go and relax or garden in summer. It was also a source of a lot of fruits and vegetables. Imagine even the poorest families owning land... And the factory my grandfather worked at was donating materials to build a house to every worker, so he built a house himself.

Grape flavor in candy is meant to replicate a specific type of grapes called concord grapes. The variety we all eat and get from the grocery store are called table grapes, and have much, much less flavor. Concord grapes are said to taste "like an expolsion in your mouth", which is what candy makers were going for when they made grape flavor.

The relationship between flavors and colors varies a lot between countries.  For example, in Japan, green items are usually Melon flavored (not sour apple or lime).  And Blue items are usually "Soda" or "Cider" flavor.  This is a hard flavor to describe, but it's a very artifical candy-like flavor, similar to some cotton candy or, yes, some cheap soda.  Purple is usually grape and yellow is usually Lemon, but red can be almost anything, with cherry, strawberry and  others (Fruit punch, apple, etc.).  All in all, if you moved to Japan and chose junk foods based on color, you'd frequently be surprised.

so blue raspberry is actually a chemical in raspberries that has that raspberr-iness. HOWEVER a single chemical does not a fruit make and thus it tastes nothing like one because it is only part of the story like all the aromatic compounds and other complexities of taste. Many fruits have a major flavour chemical that has the most fruitness of that fruit but lacks a lot of what I described above ie grape which is mostly floral and scent hence why grape flavour doesnt taste like it even though that chemical is identical. As someone with gustatory-visual synesthesia (meaning I can taste colors) I can confirm that the particular shade of blue used for blue raspberry flavoring tastes exactly like it's supposed to. Additionally, artificial grape flavoring tastes like the color purple, green apple tastes like green, and fruit punch tastes like the color red. Chemical engineers have figured out ways to turn unnaturally vibrant colors into something that people are able to taste and have strong associations to, and I've always found it so fascinating that there are ways to simulate what I experience on a daily basis.

Actually GRAPES don't taste like grapes. The giant things you buy to put in your kids' lunch-boxes have little resemblance to the way grapes actually taste, mostly because they grow them so freaking big they're mostly water (a problem with most modern produce). If you try grapes from a vineyard growing for wine-making (where the sugars need to remain high for fermentation) you can taste the actual "grape" flavor. And grape candy is closer than you might think.

Commenting before watching the video, trying to predict the final conclusion from 5 minutes of googling: Blue raspberries exist: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubus_leucodermis Too many things are red, processed sweet food companies needed to visually differentiate cherry from strawberry from raspberry from watermelon. Since blue raspberries exist, it made sense to give them the color blue even though the "blue raspberry" flavor wasn't isolated from them or made to mimic them particularly closely.

that grape flavor theory should go along with a banana flavor theory--  They're both interesting (and similar) stories about how much artificial flavors have changed with time-- or rather, how much they haven't actually changed at all. Grape flavoring is inspired by Concord grapes, while Banana flavoring is actually inspired by a now dead/extinct strain of banana called the 'Gros Michel' that was far sweeter, as opposed to modern 'Cavendish' bananas' more reserved taste.  The entire sort of story of artificial flavoring (especially the flavors that are now main staples due to what fruits were popular at the time) is so fascinating to me

Was this ever a mystery? Pretty sure everyone knows blue raspberry is just raspberry flavour paired with blue food dye. The main reason raspberry flavour is blue in many candies is to distinguish them from already established red and pink varieties like cherry, strawberry and watermelon. If you have both flavours in the same bag, and they're both red, no one can tell them apart.

I'm from Canada, where we have tutti-frutti and blue raspberry, and the tutti-futti is colored pinkish. Usually it's a pink/orange striped affair. Not bright pink or orange, softer almost pastel. And it has a VERY district smell/taste from blue raspberry items. Keeping in mind that almost all taste is smell, and that scent molecules, even of the same name can smell different whether they are "forwards" or "backwards", so it's possible they are the same mixture of molecules in different formations, but there would be no mix up if you handed them to people to taste. They taste very different. But the cough syrup thing people have commented, both does and doesn't make sense. In Japan I have heard their medicine tastes like Root Beer so they don't usually have root beer candies. But in North America we have grape cough syrup, have had it for ages. Heck our most famous medicine is that banana flavored one for kids and that flavor is still hugely popular in candies. So maybe the medicine flavor similarities are not impacting things the way you guys think.

Fun fact: Morpho butterflies don't have blue pigment. They aren't truly blue despite looking that way. They're actually brown, but they have fancy scales that scatter light to look blue despite having no pigment. It's called structural coloration.  Blue eyes also don't have blue pigment. It's all just fancy light tricks.   It kinda works like soap bubbles. Like soap bubbles obviously don't have pigments, so they're transparent, but they look colourful because how they scatter light.

Before I actually watch this, here's what I read a while ago. It's not that they wanted to come up with a flavor for blue and choose raspberry, it's actually the opposite. Since there are already so many red fruit flavors, they chose to make raspberry flavored things blue instead to dry it apart from all the other red things. In other words, blue is simply the color chosen for things raspberry flavored sure to there being so many other things that are red.

I'm gonna have to nitpick: the blue morpho butterfly does not contain any blue pigment. Like all butterflies its wings are covered in tiny scales. The scales of the blue morpho basically act as tiny prisims, refracting the light that hits them so that it appears blue. So when matpat said that blue is an extremely rare naturally occurring pigment, he was correct, but the wings of the blue morpho butterfly are blue for the same reason that water and the sky appear to be blue.  As I said, I'm nitpicking, but I work in a butterfly museum and am surrounded  by morphos all day and tell people this exact same thing. Hopefully this comment was educational for some people.
